                    }
                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome-wide analysis of Cushion willow provides insights into alpine plant divergence in a biodiversity hotspot"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-11-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The Hengduan Mountains (HDM) biodiversity hotspot exhibits exceptional alpine plant diversity. Here, we investigate factors driving intraspecific divergence within a HDM alpine species Salix brachista (Cushion willow), a common component of subnival assemblages. We produce a high-quality genome assembly for this species and characterize its genetic diversity, population structure and pattern of evolution by resequencing individuals collected across its distribution. We detect population divergence that has been shaped by a landscape of isolated sky island-like habitats displaying strong environmental heterogeneity across elevational gradients, combined with population size fluctuations that&#160;have occurred since approximately the late Miocene. These factors are likely important drivers of intraspecific divergence within Cushion willow and possibly other alpine plants with a similar distribution. Since intraspecific divergence is often the first step toward speciation, the same factors can be important contributors to the high alpine species diversity in the HDM. Exceptional alpine plant diversity exists in the Hengduan Mountains. Here, through genome assembly and population genomics studies, the authors find notable intraspecific divergence among Cushion willow populations isolated by the sky island-like habitats and consider it contributes to speciation and biodiversity."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The Hengduan Mountains (HDM) biodiversity hotspot exhibits exceptional alpine plant diversity. Here, we investigate factors driving intraspecific divergence within a HDM alpine species <i>Salix brachista</i> (Cushion willow), a common component of subnival assemblages. We produce a high<b>-</b>quality genome assembly for this species and characterize its genetic diversity, population structure and pattern of evolution by resequencing individuals collected across its distribution. We detect population divergence that has been shaped by a landscape of isolated sky island-like habitats displaying strong environmental heterogeneity across elevational gradients, combined with population size fluctuations thatÂ have occurred since approximately the late Miocene. These factors are likely important drivers of intraspecific divergence within Cushion willow and possibly other alpine plants with a similar distribution. Since intraspecific divergence is often the first step toward speciation, the same factors can be important contributors to the high alpine species diversity in the HDM.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Hengduan Mountains (HDM) biodiversity hotspot is part of the Tibeto-Himalayan region (THR), which also comprises the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau (QTP) and the Himalayas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Mountains as evolutionary arenas: patterns, emerging approaches, paradigm shifts, and their implications for plant phylogeographic research in the Tibeto-Himalayan Region. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 195 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3257e855">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Favre, A. et al. The role of the uplift of the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau for the evolution of Tibetan biotas. Biol. Rev. Camb. Philos. Soc. 90, 236â253 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3257e858">2</a></sup>. Within the HDM a series of parallel, mostly north-south oriented high mountains separated by deep valleys occurs, with elevations ranging from 1000âm on valley floors to &gt;6000âm on mountain peaks (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1</a>). The region possesses exceptional species richness (~12,800 seed plant species are recognized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Sun, H., Zhang, J., Deng, T. &amp; Boufford, D. E. Origins and evolution of plant diversity in the Hengduan Mountains, China. Plant Divers 39, 161â166 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3257e865">3</a></sup>), with many different alpine plants present in the subnival zone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Favre, A. et al. The role of the uplift of the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau for the evolution of Tibetan biotas. Biol. Rev. Camb. Philos. Soc. 90, 236â253 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3257e869">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wen, J., Zhang, J. Q., Nie, Z. L., Zhong, Y. &amp; Sun, H. Evolutionary diversifications of plants on the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau. Front. Genet. 5, 4 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3257e872">4</a></sup>. This has motivated recent interest in understanding the origin and evolution of biodiversity in the QTP and within the HDM in particular<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Favre, A. et al. The role of the uplift of the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau for the evolution of Tibetan biotas. Biol. Rev. Camb. Philos. Soc. 90, 236â253 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3257e876">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, J. Q., Duan, Y. W., Hao, G., Ge, X. J. &amp; Sun, H. Evolutionary history and underlying adaptation of alpine plants on the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau. J. Syst. Evol. 52, 241â249 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3257e879">5</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="339" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Cushion willow distribution pattern and locations of population samples. <b>a</b> Female individual with flowers. <b>b</b> Male individual with flowers. <b>c</b> Low elevation Cushion willow population located on open river bank at an elevation of 2950 m above sea level. <b>d</b> High elevation Cushion willow population on a scree slope at an elevation of 4000âm. <b>e</b> Sample locations of the 14 Cushion willow populations in HDM and nearby area of the eastern Himalaya. Pie charts at sampling locations indicate the distribution of genetic groups identified by a StructureÂ analysis of the population. <b>f</b> Scheme of sky island distribution pattern</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>One hypothesis for the rich biodiversity found in mountainous regions like the HDM is uplift-driven diversification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hughes, C. E. &amp; Atchison, G. W. The ubiquity of alpine plant radiations: from the Andes to the Hengduan Mountains. New Phytol. 207, 275â282 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3257e914">6</a></sup>; that is, orogeny produced topographical features creating diverse habitats and isolated populations, facilitating rapid intraspecific divergence leading to in situ speciation. In addition, the connectivity of populations could have been influenced by Quaternary climate fluctuations, contributing also to population divergence and speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Mountains as evolutionary arenas: patterns, emerging approaches, paradigm shifts, and their implications for plant phylogeographic research in the Tibeto-Himalayan Region. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 195 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3257e918">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ebersbach, J. et al. Driving forces behind evolutionary radiations: Saxifraga section Ciliatae (Saxifragaceae) in the region of the QinghaiâTibet Plateau. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 186, 304â320 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3257e921">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ebersbach, J., Schnitzler, J., Favre, A. &amp; Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Evolutionary radiations in the species-rich mountain genus Saxifraga L. BMC Evol. Biol. 17, 119 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3257e924">8</a></sup>. However, the relatively few studies aimed at testing these hypotheses have employed a limited number of molecular markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Favre, A. et al. The role of the uplift of the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau for the evolution of Tibetan biotas. Biol. Rev. Camb. Philos. Soc. 90, 236â253 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3257e928">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wen, J., Zhang, J. Q., Nie, Z. L., Zhong, Y. &amp; Sun, H. Evolutionary diversifications of plants on the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau. Front. Genet. 5, 4 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3257e931">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, J. Q., Duan, Y. W., Hao, G., Ge, X. J. &amp; Sun, H. Evolutionary history and underlying adaptation of alpine plants on the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau. J. Syst. Evol. 52, 241â249 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3257e934">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ebersbach, J. et al. Driving forces behind evolutionary radiations: Saxifraga section Ciliatae (Saxifragaceae) in the region of the QinghaiâTibet Plateau. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 186, 304â320 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3257e937">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ebersbach, J., Schnitzler, J., Favre, A. &amp; Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Evolutionary radiations in the species-rich mountain genus Saxifraga L. BMC Evol. Biol. 17, 119 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3257e940">8</a></sup>. Here we report a population genomics analysis to examine the genetic structure, demographic history and genetic basis of adaptation to subnival conditions of a typical subnival species of <i>Salix</i> (willow) that occurs in the HDM. Our aim was to determine which factors shaped the genetic structure of this species, providing insights into the factors that could have also potentially driven intraspecific divergence within other alpine species in the HDM, and thus contributed to the high alpine plant diversity within this region.</p><p>The dioecious genus <i>Salix</i> (Salicaceae) exhibits high species richness in the HDM, with approximately 152 species recognized, accounting for 57% and 27% of total <i>Salix</i> species distributed in China and worldwide, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Ding, T. Origin, divergence and geographical distribution of Salicaceae. Acta Bot. Yunnan 17, 277â290 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3257e956">9</a></sup>. Members of the genus are important components of alpine and temperate vegetation. <i>Salix brachista</i> Schneid is a typical alpine willow mainly distributed in the subnival zones of mountains in the HDM, and also occasionally in neighboring areas (i.e., the eastern Himalaya and middle Yunnan Plateau), where it is a dominant component of many subnival assemblages. Morphologically, it is a cushion plant (hereafter referred to as Cushion willow) with a creeping stem and lateral branches growing to a height of usually no more than 5âcm (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1aâd</a>). It is recognized as a sky island<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Heald, W. Sky islands of Arizona. Nat. Hist. 60, 56â63 (1951)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3257e967">10</a></sup> plant in the HDM, with populations distributed at elevations of around 4000âm on mountain peaks isolated by forests andÂ low-lying terrain below. Occasionally, it is also found at lower elevations, for example in open habitats on river banks at ~3000âm, amidst high altitude forest (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1c, e, f</a>).</p><p>Here, we report a high<b>-</b>quality genome sequence of Cushion willow, which is used as a reference for aÂ population genomics study of 77 individuals collected from 14 populations across the speciesâ distribution. We then characterize the speciesâÂ genetic diversity, population structure, demographic history, and footprints of adaptive evolution to the alpine environment. We interpret the results in the context of explaining rapid intraspecific divergence leading to high plant species richness within the HDM.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>We sequenced the genome of a female Cushion willow plant with a nuclear genome size estimated to be ~421âMb and ~400âMb by flow cytometry (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">1</a>) and <i>K</i>-mer analysis (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">1</a>), respectively. This plant was originally collected from the Tianbao Mountain in Shangri-La County, Yunnan, China, and subsequently maintained within the Kunming Botanical Garden. Cytogenetic studies detected 38 chromosomes, indicating that the sequenced individual is diploid (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â38) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">2</a>). We generated 17.5âGb (~119 million reads) of Illumina PCR-free short reads, 50âGb (~6.8 million reads, average 7.3âkb) of PacBio single-molecule long reads, and 29.69âGb of Oxford Nanopore Technologies (ONT) long reads (for statistics of ONT reads see Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">2</a>), corresponding to approximately 43Ã, 125Ã, and 74Ã coverage of the whole genome, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">3</a>). We used Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1017">11</a></sup> pre-corrected ONT reads for genome assembly by SMARTdenovo. This assembly was polished for two and five rounds based on PacBio long reads and Illumina short reads, respectively, to generate a 351.67âMb genome assembly with 245 contigs (contig N50â=â5.34âMb) (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">4</a>). The ~50âMb of unassembled genomic sequence consisted mainly of highly repetitive sequences, possibly from centromeres and telomeres (see Methods for details). In contrast, protein-coding regions were assembled to near completeness as shown by BUSCO evaluation below.</p><p>Using Hi-C (in vitro fixation of chromosomes) data, 337.28âMb (99.32%) of the contig sequences were anchored to 19 pseudo-chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">3</a>), which corresponds to the haploid chromosome number of the species. We proceeded to running gap closure and removing redundancy, before further polishing the assembly with two additional runs of Illumina short reads. The final reference assembly was 339.59âMb in length and included 78 contigs (contig N50â=â9.52âMb) and 19 pseudo-chromosomes (scaffold N50â=â17.92âMb). The length of pseudo-chromosomes ranged from 11.69âMb to 39.69âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">5</a>). The chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes were assembled into circular DNA molecules of 155,604 bp and 608,983 bp, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">5</a>). We detected a total of 2,419,503 heterozygous sites (comprising 2,133,443 SNPs and 286,060 indels), which corresponds to a heterozygosity rate of approximately 0.71%. About 98.49% of Illumina short reads were mapped successfully back to the genome assembly, and about 98.82% of the assembly was covered by at least 10Ã reads, indicating that the current assembly covered most unique genomic regions and is highly accurate at the single-nucleotide level (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="351" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Cushion willow evolution. <b>a</b> Circos plot showing the genomic features of <i>Salix brachista</i>. The features highlighted from innermost to outermost circle are (i) intra-genome collinear blocks connected by curved lines, (ii) GC content (30%â50%), (iii) SNP density, (iv) gene density (0â50 per window), (v) distribution of Class II TE (scale: 0%â5%), (vi) distribution of Class I TE (scale: 0%â5%). All statistics are computed for windows of 200âkb. <b>b</b> Gene family evolution of Cushion willow. The phylogenetic tree was constructed from sequences of 3040 single-copy genes shared by ingroup Sal-SP species. The number of gene families that expanded (turquoise), contracted (magenta) and were rapidly evolving (blue) in each lineage after speciation are indicated above the corresponding branch. <b>c</b> Gene collinearity between <i>S. brachista</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. The <i>x</i>-axis and <i>y</i>-axis correspond to the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>S. brachista</i> chromosomes, respectively. <b>d</b> Collinearity between chromosome 1 and 16 of <i>S. brachista</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the Cushion willow genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene and repeat annotations</h3><p>Our assembly indicated that 141.4âMb (41.65%) of the assembled genome consisted of repeated regions (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Tab1">1</a>). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most abundant annotations, making up 18.76% of the genome, with Gypsy and Copia elements accounting for 9.44% and 9.15% of the genome, respectively, (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM5">1</a>). <i>Copia</i> elements comprised a higher proportion of the genome of <i>S. brachista</i> relative to that found in other Salicaceae species examined (3.71â6.38%, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">7</a>), indicating a considerable expansion of this element in the Cushion willow genome. For <i>Gypsy</i> the situation was more complex, with contraction occurring in the genomes of all three <i>Salix</i> species relative to estimates of genome representation in <i>Populus</i>, although this contraction was less marked in Cushion willow than in the other two <i>Salix</i> species examined. A phylogenetic analysis of <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> sequences across all six Salicaceae species revealed no evidence for any species-specific family members (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">5</a>), demonstrating that <i>Copia</i>/<i>Gypsy</i> sequence divergence predates species divergence.</p><p>We masked repeated regions and proceeded to annotate the genome using a comprehensive strategy combining evidence-based and <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction. Following the Maker pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1273">12</a></sup>, we incorporated protein sequences from five Salicaceae species and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, and transcripts assembled from Cushion willow RNA-seq data (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">8</a><b>)</b>. In total, 30,209 gene models were identified, with an average CDS length of 1.31âkb and an average of 5.83 exons per transcript. The gene, exon and CDS region spanned 32.2% (109.3âMb), 14.4% (49.0âMb), and 11.7% (39.6âMb) of the assembled genome, respectively. In addition, we identified 118 ribosomal RNA (rRNA) and 784 transfer RNA (tRNA) sequences. Most of the predicted proteins (97.4%) matched a predicted protein in public databases (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">9</a>). The predominant biological processes among annotated gene models included cellular processes (7% of gene models) and responses to stimuli (5% of gene models) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>We used BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1295">13</a></sup> to evaluate the quality of our gene annotation and found that 1383 out of the 1440 (96.1%) highly conserved core proteins in the Embryophyta lineage were present in our gene annotation, of which 1235 (85.8%) were single-copy genes and 148 (10.3%) were duplicated. For the remaining conserved genes, 143 (1.0%) had fragmented matches and 43 (2.9%) were missing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative genomics and whole genome duplication events</h3><p>To investigate the evolution of Cushion willow, we compared its genome to those of nine other Malpighiales species. These included six Salicaceae species â <i>Populus alba</i> var. <i>pyramidalis</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa, P. deltoides, P. euphratica, Salix purpurea, S. suchowensis</i>, and three other species - <i>Linum usitatissimum</i> (Linaceae), <i>Manihot esculenta</i> (Euphorbiaceae), and <i>Ricinus communis</i> (Euphorbiaceae).</p><p>We clustered the annotated genes of all ten Malpighiales species (Mlp-SP) into orthologous and paralogous groups using Orthfinder2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Emms D. M., Kelly S. OrthoFinder2: Fast and accurate phylogenomic orthology analysis from gene sequences. Preprint at &#xA;https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/466201v1&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1329">14</a></sup> and identified 13,793 and 25,467 orthologous clusters shared by the seven Salicaceae species (Sal-SP) and by the Mlp-SP group, respectively, of which 3040 and 518 were single-copy orthologs, respectively. Phylogenetic trees (using <i>Manihot esculenta</i> as outgroup) constructed from these single-copy orthologs confirmed that Salicaceae <i>s.str</i>., <i>Salix</i> and <i>Populus</i> are monophyletic clades (trees for Mlp-SP and Sal-SP groups are shown in Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig2">2b</a>, respectively), in accordance with our previous phylogenetic studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chen, J. H., Sun, H., Wen, J. &amp; Yang, Y. P. Molecular phylogeny of Salix L. (Salicaceae) inferred from three chloroplast datasets and its systematic implications. Taxon 59, 29â37 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1352">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Cushion willow predicted gene models were clustered into 19,032 gene families, with 12,163 singleton families and 6869 multi-copy families. Furthermore, we identified 13,793 gene families shared by Sal-SP species, 2262 species-specific families, and 5239 Cushion willow genes that did not cluster with orthologous clusters. Gene family analysis revealed that 866 of the 13,793 gene families shared among the Sal-SP species had undergone expansion in Cushion willow, 1446 gene families experienced contraction, and 132 gene families displayed signatures of rapid evolution (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><p>To identify whole genome duplication (WGD) events, we used the density distribution of synonymous substitution rates per site (<i>K</i>s) between collinear paralogous genes, with the assumption that level of synonymous substitutions between two homologous sequences increases approximately linearly with time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1368">16</a></sup>. A total of 7464 syntenic blocks were identified in the Cushion willow genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig2">2a</a>). The total length of these blocks was 280.6âMb (82.6% of the assembly), suggesting that the majority of the Cushion willow genome was duplicated during its evolution. The <i>K</i>s distribution of collinear gene pairs confirmed the common WGD event shared by both <i>Populus</i> and <i>Salix</i> genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1385">17</a></sup>, indicated by a <i>K</i>s of around 0.3 and 0.25 in <i>Salix</i> and <i>Populus</i> species, respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">8</a>). The different <i>K</i>s values possibly reflect different nucleotide substitution rates in <i>Populus</i> and <i>Salix</i> lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Dai, X. G. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1411">18</a></sup>.</p><p>We further performed synteny and collinearity analyses based on Salicaceae species with chromosomal scale genome assemblies. We found high synteny between <i>Salix</i> and <i>Populus</i> species (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">9a, b</a>), supporting the hypothesis that these genera share a common WGD in their evolutionary history. Some distinct chromosomal rearrangement events were detected in chromosomes 4, 15, and 19 between Cushion willow and <i>S. purpurea</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">9a</a>). Chromosome 15 contains the sex-determination locus in <i>Salix</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou, R. et al. Characterization of a large sex determination region in Salix purpurea L. (Salicaceae). Mol. Genet. Genomics 293, 1437â1452 (2018)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1437">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hou, J. et al. Different autosomes evolved into sex chromosomes in the sister genera of Salix and Populus. Sci. Rep. UK 5, 9076 (2015)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1437_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Pucholt, P., Wright, A. E., Conze, L. L., Mank, J. E. &amp; Berlin, S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1991â2001 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1440">21</a></sup>, which raises the possibility of the sex determination region undergoing evolution in the <i>Salix</i> lineage. Interestingly, we found that chromosome 16 of Cushion willow involved conjunction of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> chromosome 16 and the lower portion of chromosome 1, while chromosome 1 of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> was a fusion of Cushion willow chromosome 1 and the lower portion of chromosome 16 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig2">2c, d</a>). The same pattern of chromosomal restructuring was detected between Cushion willow and <i>P. deltoides</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">9c</a>) and was previously reported between <i>S. purpurea</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhou, R. et al. Characterization of a large sex determination region in Salix purpurea L. (Salicaceae). Mol. Genet. Genomics 293, 1437â1452 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1468">19</a></sup>, as well as between <i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hou, J. et al. Major chromosomal rearrangements distinguish willow and poplar after the ancestral &#34;Salicoid&#34; genome duplication. Genome 8, 1868â1875 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1478">22</a></sup>. This indicates that the <i>Populus</i> and <i>Salix</i> lineages experienced different chromosomal fission and fusion events after they diverged from their common ancestor, as proposed by Hou et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hou, J. et al. Major chromosomal rearrangements distinguish willow and poplar after the ancestral &#34;Salicoid&#34; genome duplication. Genome 8, 1868â1875 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1488">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Temporal origin of Cushion willow</h3><p>Divergence time estimation with fossil calibration based on 390 single-copy genes indicated when Cushion willow diverged from its close relative, <i>Salix souliei</i>, a dwarf willow (also placed in section <i>Lindleyanae</i>). <i>Salix souliei</i> is endemic to the THR and is confined to subnival regions above 4000âm, though is more widely distributed than Cushion willow, occurring in the QTP as well as the HDM and Himalaya. Our results indicate that the two species diverged ~11.73 Ma (6.83â18.34âMa) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">10</a>), suggesting that Cushion willow must have evolved after the split with <i>S. souliei</i> and almost certainly less than 11.73 Ma. The Himalaya underwent progressive uplift during the middle Miocene (~13âMa), with southern parts of the QTP likely reaching elevations comparable to those at present by mid-to-late Miocene (~5â15âMa). In contrast, orogeny of the HDM occurred mainly after the late Miocene and reached peak elevation shortly before the Late Pliocene (~2.6âMa, reviewed by Favre et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Favre, A. et al. The role of the uplift of the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau for the evolution of Tibetan biotas. Biol. Rev. Camb. Philos. Soc. 90, 236â253 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1516">2</a></sup>). It is feasible, therefore, that the ancestor of Cushion willow diverged from that of <i>S. souliei</i> in the Himalaya or QTP, and then dispersed to the eastern Himalaya or HDM, to give rise to Cushion willow. This kind of origin and diversification, possibly associated with tectonic events within the THR and the formation of climate-induced divergent habitats, has been suggested for several other species and species groups in the HDM, including <i>Saussurea</i> species, <i>Rhododendron</i> subg. <i>Hymenanthes</i>, and <i>Rhodiola</i> species (reviewed by Wen et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wen, J., Zhang, J. Q., Nie, Z. L., Zhong, Y. &amp; Sun, H. Evolutionary diversifications of plants on the Qinghai-Tibetan Plateau. Front. Genet. 5, 4 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1536">4</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genomic footprints of alpine adaptation</h3><p>We tested for enrichment of gene ontology terms (GO) and pathways (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes, KEGG) by comparing the annotation content of expanded, rapidly evolved and species-specific genes in Cushion willow to the full complement of genes in its genome (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">10</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM6">2</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM8">4</a>). Most notably, the top two enriched KEGG pathways among expanded gene families in <i>Salix brachista</i> were base excision repair and flavonoid biosynthesis (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">10</a>). High elevations are characterized by high levels of UV radiation, which may cause DNA damage. Thus, given that base excision repair is a predominant DNA damage repair pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Palomera-Sanchez, Z. &amp; Zurita, M. Open, repair and close again: Chromatin dynamics and the response to UV-induced DNA damage. DNA Repair 10, 119â125 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1565">23</a></sup>, it is possible that the expansion of gene families with functions related to DNA damage repair in Cushion willow were driven by natural selection in high elevation environments. This pathway was not enriched in expanded genes families in either of the two non-alpine <i>Salix</i> species examined (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM9">5</a>). In addition, the finding that flavonoid biosynthesis, in particular anthocyanin synthesis, was over-represented is also consistent with adaptation to high elevation environments. Flavonoids, such as the common red, blue and purple anthocyanin pigments of plant tissues, are ubiquitous plant secondary products, which due to their UV-absorbing properties, have long been considered important in UV protection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Winkel-Shirley, B. Biosynthesis of flavonoids and effects of stress. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 5, 218â223 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1575">24</a></sup>. In Cushion willow, stems, branches and branchlets are typically purple in color, indicative of anthocyanin accumulation in these organs, which we confirmed by anthocyanin concentration measurements (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">11</a>). Some GO-terms related to flavonoid biosynthesis were also enriched, including anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase activity (GO:0047213) and anthocyanin-containing compound biosynthetic process (GO:0009718) (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM6">2</a>). We further found that compared with the two non-alpine <i>Salix</i> species examined, Cushion willow tended to have a greater number of genes from gene families exhibiting the enriched terms mentioned above (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM10">6</a>), thus reinforcing the idea that these functions may be involved in the adaptation of <i>S. brachista</i> to high elevations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Population diversification in Cushion willow</h3><p>Cushion willow is mainly distributed in the HDM and its peripheral areas, including the eastern Himalaya (Bomi county) and two high mountains in the Central Yunnan Plateau (Eryuan county and Dongchuan county) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1e</a>). We collected and sequenced 77 individuals from 14 locations covering major habitats throughout the species distribution (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1e</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM11">7</a>). From individuals of each population, we generated average 22.1-fold coverage (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM12">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM13">9</a>). After stringent variant calling and filtering, we identified a total of 1,595,067 high-quality single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) with a minor allele frequency (MAF) &gt;5% and missing frequency &lt;20%, which were used for subsequent population-based analyses (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><p>Analysis of population structure revealed seven distinct clusters (<i>K</i>â=â7) that reflected geographic divergence, and limited gene flow between certain populations (Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1</a>e and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">12</a>). Only nine individuals were of mixed ancestry, representing possible hybrids between individuals assigned to different clusters. With <i>K</i>â=â3, populations in the center of the distribution range (SL, LJ, and DQ) clustered together, indicating their very close relationship (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">12</a>). Similar conclusions regarding genetic divergence within Cushion willow are drawn from a neighbor-joining (NJ) tree based on the ~1.6 million high-quality SNPs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3b</a>), and also from principal component analysis (PCA) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3c</a>). In the NJ tree, populations were resolved as robust monophyletic clades (bootstrap value of 100%), except for those located in Lijiang county (LJ_XF, LJ_LS, LS_BS and LJ_GH, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1b</a>). These four populations, which occurred in close geographical proximity on the same mountain, formed one robust clade. They also formed a single cluster in structure analyses with <i>K</i> ranging from 4 to 10 (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">12</a>), confirming their close relationship.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="762" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Population genetic strucure and demographic history. <b>a</b> Genetic structure of Cushion willow. The length of each colored segment represents the proportion of the individualâs genome from <i>K</i>â=â7 ancestral genetic groups. The populations grouped by county individual identifiers are indicated along the <i>x</i>-axis. <b>b</b> Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of Cushion willow based on SNPs from whole-genome resequencing. Branch lengths are scaled to genetic similarity (<i>p</i> distance, see scale bar). <b>c</b> PCA plot of Cushion willow genetic variation. The fractions of the variance explained by eigenvector 1 and 2 are 11.0% and 9.7%, respectively. Each shaded area groups populations from the same region: pink for populations from northwest Yunnan at the center of the area sampled, blue for eastern peripheral populations, and green for the west most peripheral populations. <b>d</b> Violin plot of Tajimaâs <i>D</i> in whole genome of <i>Salix brachista</i>. The width depicts a 90<sup>0</sup>-rotated kernel density trace and its reflection. Vertical black boxes denote the interquartile range between 25 and 75 percentiles, and the white point inside denotes the median. <i>n</i>â=â168,546. <b>e</b> Detection of gene flow between Cushion willow populations. Lines represent gene flow, with arrows indicating the direction of gene flow. The horizontal scale bar at the bottom (drift parameter) shows a tenfold average standard error of the entries in the sample covariance matrix. The color scale shows the migration weight: red denotes strong gene flow, while yellow denotes weak gene flow. <b>f</b> Stairway plot showing historical changes in effective population size (<i>y</i>-axis) for LJ population with a generation time of 10 years. Red, dark gray, and light gray lines denote the medians, 12.5 and 87.5 percentiles, 2.5 and 97.5 percentiles of population sizes, respectively. The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3d</a> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Interestingly, one individual from the western most population (BM5) clustered with the distant southeastern population (DC) some 792âkm away, suggesting the possibility of long<b>-</b>distance dispersal (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3a, b</a>). Willow seeds are covered in cottony hair and can be dispersed by wind over long distances<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Alsos, I. G. et al. Frequent long-distance plant colonization in the changing Arctic. Science 316, 1606â1609 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1723">25</a></sup>. Similarly, long-distance dispersal is feasible via pollen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Perdereau, A., Kelleher, C. T., Douglas, G. C. &amp; Hodkinson, T. R. High levels of gene flow and genetic diversity in Irish populations of Salix caprea L. inferred from chloroplast and nuclear SSR markers. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 202 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1727">26</a></sup>. It is possible that long-distance wind dispersal of Cushion willow seeds and pollen is aided by the monsoon system in the HDM and the eastern Himalaya.</p><p>A population graph (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3e</a>) inferred by Treemix<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pickrell, J. K. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002967 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1737">27</a></sup> indicated similar relationships between populations to those revealed in the NJ tree (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3b</a>) and low levels of gene flow except between populations LJ_XF and LJ_GH (0.56) and DC and DY (0.37). The latter estimate should be treated with caution, given that the DY population comprised a single individual, possibly a hybrid of DC and MN parents, that may have established at the DY location following rare long-distance seed dispersal. Overall, gene flow tended to be more frequent between nearby populations than distant ones, as confirmed by a Pearson correlation coefficient analysis of population differentiation (fixation index, <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) against geographical distance (<i>p</i>â=â0.00000127) (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM14">10</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig4">4a, b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13128-y/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13128_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="530" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Signatures of selection in Cushion willow sampled from high and lower elevation sites. <b>a</b> Pairwise <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> between populations (<i>y</i>-axis) as a function of the pairwise spatial distance between populations (<i>x</i>-axis). Each point represents one population pair. The solid blue line represents a significant linear relationship; parameter estimates for the linear regression, as well as Pearsonâs correlation coefficient and associated <i>p</i>-value are given at the top-right corner of panel <b>b</b>. Heatmap of pairwise <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> estimates between populations. <b>c</b> Patterns of linkage disequilibrium (LD). LD (<i>y</i>-axis) decays as a function of genomic distance between polymorphisms (<i>x</i>-axis) in high elevation (solid red line) and low elevation (solid blue line) Cushion willows populations. LD was measured by <i>r</i><sup>2</sup>. <b>d</b> Manhattan plots of CLR among the high and low elevation Cushion willow plants of LJ populations. Dashed line indicates CLR value threshold. Genes located within the significant CLR peaks and corresponding annotations are denoted</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Divergence and demographic history related to climate change</h3><p>To obtain an indication of the timing of intraspecific divergence within Cushion willow, we used fastsimcoal 2.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Excoffier, L., Dupanloup, I., Huerta-Sanchez, E., Sousa, V. C. &amp; Foll, M. Robust demographic inference from genomic and SNP data. PLoS Genet. 9, e1003905 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1815">28</a></sup>, a coalescent simulation-based method, to infer the demographic histories and divergence times of six of the genetically divergent groups (populations) identified by structure analysis, including both centrally (DQ, SL, LJ) and peripherally (BM, MN, DC) distributed ones (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1e</a>), based on their joint site frequency spectrum (SFS). Twenty-two alternative historical divergence models were fitted to the SFS (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">13</a>) with a mutation rate of 1.01Ã10<sup>â8</sup> mutations per site per generation assumed. Divergence times estimated for generation times of 3, 10, and 15 years (see Methods) approximated 0.42â2.1âMa between peripherally and centrally distributed populations, which were older than those estimated between centrally distributed populations, which approximated 0.18â1.1âMa (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM15">11</a>). Thus, all splits of these populations (between which gene flow is very weak, regardless of geographical distance, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM15">11</a>) were estimated to have occurred after the uplift of the HDM. Their divergence was most likely driven by isolation on different mountain summits (sky islands), and possibly adaptation to different subnival habitats and/or recurrent cycles of mixing, isolation and mixing (see below).</p><p>We employed the stairway plot method based on SNP frequency spectra to infer changes in population size (<i>Ne</i>) over time for six of the genetic groups identified by our structure analysis of Cushion willow. Again, a mutation rate of 1.01âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> mutations per generation for generation times of 3, 10, and 15 years was assumed. Similar curves for changes in population size within each genetic group were obtained across the three generation times (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3f</a>, Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">14</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">16</a>); however, the number and timing of changes in population size differed among genetic groups. Such variation in demographic history between these groups is not unexpected given that glaciation of the THR region was highly variable, with extensive micro-climatic variation within individual mountain ranges<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Mountains as evolutionary arenas: patterns, emerging approaches, paradigm shifts, and their implications for plant phylogeographic research in the Tibeto-Himalayan Region. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 195 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1852">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Owen, L. A. &amp; Dortch, J. M. Nature and timing of Quaternary glaciation in the Himalayan-Tibetan orogen. Quat. Sci. Rev. 88, 14â54 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1855">29</a></sup>.</p><p>Importantly, all population bottlenecks were estimated to have occurred since the late Miocene and mostly since the beginning of the Quaternary except for two estimated to have occurred around 8 Ma with a generation time of 15 years. Each bottleneck event was usually followed by a population expansion, reflecting major climatic fluctuations since the late Miocene and particularly over the last 3 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Mountains as evolutionary arenas: patterns, emerging approaches, paradigm shifts, and their implications for plant phylogeographic research in the Tibeto-Himalayan Region. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 195 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1862">1</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3f</a>; Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">14</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">16</a>). During glaciations, populations may have moved downslope to lower elevations, but nonetheless suffered marked reductions in effective size. During these periods, secondary contacts could have occurred between previously isolated sky island populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Hewitt, G. The genetic legacy of the Quaternary ice ages. Nature 405, 907â913 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1875">30</a></sup>, particularly neighboring ones, providing opportunities for hybridization and gene flow. This mixing would have ceased during interglacial periods when populations moved upslope and became isolated again on different sky islands. Recently, it has been shown that recurrent cycles of admixture and geographic isolation before complete reproductive isolation is achieved can potentially lead to high rates of speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="He, Z. et al. Speciation with gene flow via cycles of isolation and migration: Insights from multiple mangrove taxa. Natl Sci. Rev. 6, 275â288 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1880">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Abbott, R. J. A mixing-isolation-mixing model of speciation can potentially explain hotspots of species diversity. Natl Sci. Rev. 6, 290â291 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1883">32</a></sup>. This process, termed the Mixing-Isolation-Mixing (MIM) model of speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="He, Z. et al. Speciation with gene flow via cycles of isolation and migration: Insights from multiple mangrove taxa. Natl Sci. Rev. 6, 275â288 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1887">31</a></sup>, could have been particularly important in mountainous regions, such as the HDM, acting as a species pump to generate high alpine plant diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Muellner-Riehl, A. N. Mountains as evolutionary arenas: patterns, emerging approaches, paradigm shifts, and their implications for plant phylogeographic research in the Tibeto-Himalayan Region. Front. Plant Sci. 10, 195 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3257e1891">1</a></sup>. Our finding that gene flow is not rare between neighboring populations of Cushion willow (Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1e</a> andÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3a, e</a>) suggests that recurrent cycles of admixture followed by isolation during the Pleistocene may have contributed to the notable genetic divergence recorded between populations of this species.</p><p>Despite fluctuations in population size, our stairway plot analysis indicated that most populations of Cushion willow expanded markedly during the Pleistocene (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">14</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">16</a>). This result is also supported by negative values of Tajimaâs <i>D</i> (â1.63, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig3">3d</a>) across the genome, a common signature of demographic expansion. There is only one population (SL_TB) that has smaller population size in most of the Pleistocene than now. This population increased in size early in the Pleistocene, but underwent significant reductions in size twice i.e. during the LGM and more recently. The DC population, which suffered a marked reduction in population size ~30âkya, nonetheless has a current size larger than the one it had at the start of the Pleistocene (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">14</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">16</a>). Taken overall, our results suggest that the species generally coped well with environmental changes caused by HDM uplift and the glacial-interglacial cycles that have occurred since the late Miocene, and that the HDM has been a long-term refugium for Cushion willow.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Selection on Cushion willow at high and lower elevations</h3><p>Cushion willow generally grows at high elevations ~4000âm, although it can also be found in open sites on river banks at lower elevations (2760â3260âm) where forest is absent (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1</a>). Significant environmental differences are evident between high and lower elevation sites (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM16">12</a>) and plants occupying these different sites are, consequently, likely to be subject to different biotic and abiotic selective pressures. To examine this possibility, we scanned the genomes of plants from high and lower elevation LJ populations located on the same mountain, using the composite likelihood ratio (CLR) test implemented in SweepFinder2 based on unfolded SFS.</p><p>Focusing on regions of the genome with CLR scores higher than thresholds estimated using neutral coalescent simulations, we identified 2119 genomic regions (2âkb sliding windows) that encompassed ~4.2â4.6âMB, containing 381 genes, and 3545 genomic regions that encompassed ~7.09âMB, containing 544 genes, that were significantly selected in high and lower elevation plants, respectively (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig4">4d</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM17">13</a>). The selective role of these regions is also supported by significantly different Tajimaâs <i>D</i> values (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">17</a>) and different linkage disequilibrium patterns (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig4">4c</a>). Although we did not detect significant GO terms with corrected <i>p</i> value &lt;0.05 for these selected genes, some notable peaks were detected across the genome characterized by consecutive blocks with very high CLR, indicating putative selective sweeps (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig4">4d</a>). Some of the genes within these peaks could, therefore, possibly be important in alpine adaptation. For example, the <i>Sabra3G0034400</i> gene located in the very notable peak of chromosome 3 in high elevation LJ populations has GO annotations that include DNA repair (GO:0006281), DNA damage induced protein phosphorylation (GO:0006975), while the <i>Sabra12G0047600</i> gene within the peak of chromosome 12 has GO annotations related to defense responses, such as defense response to bacterium (GO:0042742), and regulation of salicylic acid mediated signaling pathway (GO:2000031). In addition, of four genes located in the highest peak for lower elevation plants, <i>Sabra16G0101100</i> is a transcription factor MYB with GO annotations that include cellular response to DNA damage stimulus (GO:0006974) and response to cold (GO:0009409).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>We present here a high-quality, chromosome-scale reference genome for Cushion willow (<i>Salix brachista</i>). Whole-genome resequencing-based population genetic analyses revealed that sky island populations of Cushion willow in the HDM located on high mountains isolated from others were genetically divergent and poorly connected by gene flow, despite occasional long-distance dispersal events. Population differentiation was positively correlated with spatial distance. Genomic analysis further indicated that high and lower elevation populations of Cushion willow face different types of natural selection, and detected some genes inferred to be important to adaptation to the different environmental conditions experienced at these sites. Genetic differentiation of geographically isolated populations in combination with the different strengths of natural selection occurring between high and lower elevation populations are likely to have contributed to the divergence of Cushion willow populations in the HDM. Our results are consistent with the hypothesis that diverse isolated heterogeneous habitats on sky islands, together with climatic fluctuations that have possibly caused recurrent cycles of admixture and isolation between some populations (MIM cycles), were important drivers of intraspecific population diversification in Cushion willow, and by extension, other alpine species with similar distributions and histories in the HDM. We therefore speculate from our results that geographic isolation and environmental variation associated with elevation resulting from the uplift of the THR, in combination with the effects of climate fluctuation since the late Miocene, have contributed to the high species richness observed in the HDM by driving intraspecific population divergence, a precursor of speciation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Plant material</h3><p>The sequenced individual of Cushion willow was a female plant collected from Tianbao Mountain, Shangri-La Country, Yunnan Province, China (N27.60521Âº, E99.89144Âº), growing on a scree slope at an elevation of 4102m. Using tissue culture, we raised plantlets from this individual to facilitate isolation of long fragments of DNA.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Estimation of genome size and ploidy</h3><p>The genome size was estimated to be ~400âMb by 17-<i>k</i>-mer analysis (organelle-derived reads were removed) based on PCR-free Illumina short reads. We found large amount of high frequency (&gt;1âÃâ10<sup>5</sup>) <i>k</i>-mers in the sequenced reads (1705 kinds of 17-<i>k</i>-mers sum to ~1.626âGb), which might originate from highly repetitive regions within centromeres and telomeres, for example. These high frequency <i>k</i>-mers amounted to ~47.8âMb which, if excluded, resulted in a genome size estimated to be ~350âMb.
