The genome size estimated by 17 flow cytometry measurements was 420.83âÂ±â8.26âMb using <i>Zea mays</i> L. as a standard (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">18</a>). The sequenced individual of Cushion willow was determined as diploid with 38 chromosomes, using a cytological method described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Nemeth, A. V., Dudits, D., Molnar-Lang, M. &amp; Linc, G. Molecular cytogenetic characterisation of Salix viminalis L. using repetitive DNA sequences. J. Appl. Genet. 54, 265â269 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2027">33</a></sup> (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome sequencing</h3><p>High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from plantlets and fresh leaves using a modified CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Porebski, S., Bailey, L. G. &amp; Baum, B. R. Modification of a CTAB DNA extraction protocol for plants containing high polysaccharide and polyphenol components. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 15, 8â15 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2042">34</a></sup> for SMRT cell and Illumina sequencing, respectively. For Nanopore sequencing, PromethION libraries were prepared following the Oxford Nanopore 1D Genomic DNA by ligation (SQK-LSK109) - PromethION (version GDE_9063_v109_revD_04Jun2018) protocol, and sequenced on a Nanopore PromethION platform. For SMRT sequencing, genomic DNA was fragmented by g-Tube (Covaris, USA) and 18â22âkb DNA fragments were further purified using AMPure magnetic beads (Beckman Coulter, USA). We obtained 50âÂµg of high-quality genomic DNA with a median fragment size of 20âkb, from which we prepared sequencing libraries using the DNA Template Prep Kit 4.0 V2 (Pacific Biosciences, USA) following the manufacturerâs protocols (Pacific Biosciences, USA). We sequenced libraries on a PacBio RS II platform. For Illumina sequencing of whole-genome resequencing of individuals, short-insert libraries were constructed following the manufactureâs protocol (Illumina Inc., USA). Illumina PCR-free sequencing was also performed for the whole-genome sequenced individual, and library preparation was performed following the original Illumina TruSeq DNA PCR-free LPP (revision A, January 2013, low sample with 550 bps insert size) and sequenced on an Illumina Hiseq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNA extraction and library preparation</h3><p>To assist gene annotation, we performed RNA sequencing. We harvested whole plants of the whole-genome sequenced individual from tissue culture plantlets, including leaf, stem, and root tissues, and froze them in liquid nitrogen upon collection. Fresh leaves of four <i>Salix</i> species (<i>S. tetrasperma</i>, <i>S. babylonica</i>, <i>S. souliei</i> and <i>S. wallichiana</i>) were also collected for the purpose of divergence time estimation (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">12</a>). We performed RNA isolation immediately after collection. Total RNA was extracted with Trizol reagents and mRNAs were purified using a NEBNext Ultra RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (New England Biolabs Inc.). In total we pooled ca. in all, 2âÎ¼g of RNA for each sample to prepare the RNA-seq libraries, which we sequenced on the Illumina Hiseq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Hi-C library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>The Hi-C library was prepared following a standard procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Xie, T. et al. De novo plant genome assembly based on chromatin interactions: a case study of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Plant 8, 489â492 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2082">35</a></sup>. In brief, in situ cross-linked DNA from 700âng of high molecular-weight genomic DNAÂ was extracted and digested with a restriction enzyme. The sticky ends of the digested fragments were biotinylated, diluted, and ligated to each other randomly to form chimeric junctions. Biotinylated DNA fragments were enriched and sheared to a size of 300â500 bpÂ for preparing the sequencing library. Libraries were sequenced on a HiSeq X Ten platform (Illumina).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome assembly</h3><p>We used SMARTdenovo v1.0 (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo">https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo</a>), wtdbg2 (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg2">https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg2</a>) assemblers with ONT reads corrected by Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2102">11</a></sup> for de novo assembly. We used the following options as inputs to SMARTdenovo: -c l to generate a consensus sequence, -J 5000 to remove sequences &lt;5âkb and -k 20 to use 20-mers, as advised by the developers for large genomes. We then selected the âbestâ assembly, which was SMARTdenovo with Canu correction, based on contiguity metrics, including N50 or cumulative size (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">13</a>). Since ONT reads contain systematic errors in homopolymeric regions, we mapped PacBio long reads to the assembled genome using pbmm2 (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/pbmm2">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/pbmm2</a>) and polished two iterations by arrow (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/GenomicConsensus</a>). We then mapped Illumina reads to the polished genome assembly by BWA-MEM (<a href="https://github.com/lh3/bwa">https://github.com/lh3/bwa</a>) and polished five times using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. Plos One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2122">36</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Scaffolding with Hi-C data</h3><p>We corrected mis-joins, order and orientation of a candidate chromosome-length assembly automatically generated by 3d-DNA pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/theaidenlab/3d-dna">https://github.com/theaidenlab/3d-dna</a>), followed by anchoring contigs in the assembly. We then mapped the clean Hi-C reads to the assembly with Juicer (<a href="https://github.com/aidenlab/juicer">https://github.com/aidenlab/juicer</a>). The assembly was then manually reviewed and refined for quality control and interactive correction using Juicebox Assembly Tools (<a href="https://github.com/aidenlab/Juicebox">https://github.com/aidenlab/Juicebox</a>). In order to decrease the influence of chromosome interactions and improve the chromosome-scale assembly further, we re-scaffolded each chromosome with 3d-DNA separately, followed by manual refinement with Juicebox. We therefore anchored 19 chromosomes (337.28âMb, 99.32%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Optimization of genome assembly</h3><p>The genome assembly was gap closed twice using LR_Gapcloser (<a href="https://github.com/CAFS-bioinformatics/LR_Gapcloser">https://github.com/CAFS-bioinformatics/LR_Gapcloser</a>). We then compared the un-clustered contigs, as well as clustered contigs in Hi-C scaffolding. Contigs with identity of more than 90% (and 99% coverage for shorter contigs) were regarded as redundant sequences and were removed. Redundant sequences were mainly repeated sequences or sequences from different haplotypes. We also used the un-clustered contigs in Hi-C scaffolding to blast against the NCBI-NT database and found no contaminated contigs. Overall, we removed 80 redundant contigs, representing a total of 12.8âMb. Illumina short reads were then mapped to the genome assembly using BWA-MEM before polishing twice. The final assembly of Cushion willow was 339.59âMb in size with a contig N50 of 9.52âMb, including 19 chromosome-length scaffolds, 9 un-clustered contigs, as well as chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Transcriptome assembly</h3><p>RNA-seq reads were preprocessed by Cutadapt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Martin, M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. Embnet J. 17, 10â12 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2166">37</a></sup> to remove contaminating sequences from adapters and low base quality sequences. We mapped the RNA-seq reads to genome by HiSat2 (<a href="https://github.com/infphilo/hisat2">https://github.com/infphilo/hisat2</a>), and used StringTie (<a href="https://github.com/gpertea/stringtie">https://github.com/gpertea/stringtie</a>) for referencing-guided assembly and Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2178">38</a></sup> or genome-guided de novo assembly. Assembled transcripts were combined, with redundant ones removed (i.e. transcripts with identity of 99% and coverage of 99%), using CD-HIT (<a href="https://github.com/weizhongli/cdhit">https://github.com/weizhongli/cdhit</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Characterization of repetitive sequences</h3><p>Repeat families found in the genome assemblies of Cushion willow were independently identified de novo and classified initially using the software package RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Smit A. F. A., Hubley R., Green P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. (2013â2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2194">39</a></sup>. RepeatModerler depends on the programs RECON and RepeatScout for de novo identification of repeats within the genome assemblies. The repeat library produced by RepeatModeler was then used in the program RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Smit A. F. A., Hubley R., Green P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. (2013â2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2198">39</a></sup> to discover and identify repeats in the genome assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Gene annotation</h3><p>The Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637â644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2211">40</a></sup> ab initio gene finder was used to identify gene models for Cushion willow genome assemblies. The gene finder was trained using protein models from six species: <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Populus trichocarpa, P. euphratica</i>, <i>P. deltoides, Salix suchowensis</i>, and <i>S. purpurea</i> v1.0 (sources of genome datasets used are listed in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">14</a>). The package MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2228">12</a></sup> with Augustus was then used to annotate genes in the repeat-masked reference genome. Finally, predicted gene models with abnormal frame (no start or stop codon, or an inside stop codon) were excluded. The programs tRNAScan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2232">41</a></sup>, RNAmmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3100â3108 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2236">42</a></sup> were used to predict tRNA and rRNA, respectively, and other ncRNAs were identified by searching against the Rfam database (<a href="http://eggnogdb.embl.de/">http://eggnogdb.embl.de/</a>). Pseudogene identification was conducted using the pipeline described by Zou et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zou, C. et al. Evolutionary and expression signatures of pseudogenes in Arabidopsis and Rice. Plant Physiol. 151, 3â15 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2244">43</a></sup>. Predicted genes were aligned to proteins in SwissProt and TrEMBL databases (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/uniprot">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/uniprot</a>) using BLASTp (best hit with E &lt;10<sup>â5</sup>) to determine the best matching alignments. Motifs and functional domains were identified using InterProScan (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/InterProScan">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/InterProScan</a>) by searching against publicly available protein databases including ProDom, PROSITE, Pfam, PRINTS, and SMAR. We also mapped the predicted Cushion willow genes to their corresponding KEGG pathways, with the best hits for each gene used to establish GO and KEGG pathway.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Ortholog identification and WGD analysis</h3><p>We used Orthofinder2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Emms D. M., Kelly S. OrthoFinder2: Fast and accurate phylogenomic orthology analysis from gene sequences. Preprint at &#xA;https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/466201v1&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2267">14</a></sup> to identify homologous gene clusters (including ortholog and paralog clusters, sources of genome datasets used are listed in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">14</a>). MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2274">44</a></sup> was then used for collinear analysis. We identified homologous clusters for the aforementioned Mlp-SP and Sal-SP species groups and constructed two phylogenetic trees (using <i>Manihot esculenta</i> as outgroup) based on 518 and 3040 single-copy orthologs shared by Mlp-SP and Sal-SP groups, respectively. MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2281">45</a></sup> was used to align homologous gene pairs, before transforming aligned protein sequences into codon alignment by PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609âW612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2286">46</a></sup>. The <i>K</i>a, <i>K</i>s values of homologous gene pairs were calculated by <i>K</i>a<i>K</i>s Calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Zhang, Z. et al. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genom. Proteom. Bioinf. 4, 259â263 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2302">47</a></sup> with Yang-Nielsen (YN) model. WGD events were inferred from the distribution of <i>K</i>s.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Divergence time estimation</h3><p>Divergence times were estimated between 11 Salicaceae species, including a closely related species of Cushion willow, <i>Salix souliei</i> based on the amino acid sequences of 390 single-copy orthologous genes shared by these species. A maximum-likelihood tree was constructed by RaxML with the GAMMA BLOSUM62 model. Divergence times were estimated by MCMCtree of the PAML 4.0 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Yang, Z. H. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2321">48</a></sup> using the approximate likelihood method with independent substitution rate (clockâ=â2) and GTR substitution model, with samples drawn every 10 iterations until completion of 10<sup>7</sup> iterations. Overall, we ran 1.1âÃâ10<sup>8</sup> iterations and discarded 10<sup>7</sup> iterations as burn-in. To check for convergence to the stationary distribution, each analysis (including the codeml step) was run in duplicate with results compared between runs. Reliable fossils of <i>Populus tidwellii</i> (most likely representing a member of the stem lineage leading to <i>Populus</i> and <i>Salix</i>) and <i>Pseudosalix</i> (an extinct, early divergent member of the stem lineage of <i>Populus</i> and <i>Salix</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Manchester, S. R., Judd, W. S. &amp; Handley, B. Foliage and fruits of early poplars (Salicaceae: Populus) from the eocene of Utah, Colorado, and Wyoming. Int. J. Plant Sci. 167, 897â908 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2351">49</a></sup>, both occurred in early Middle Eocene (~48âMa). Because this divergence time has also been estimated to be 52âMa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Dai, X. G. et al. The willow genome and divergent evolution from poplar after the common genome duplication. Cell Res. 24, 1274â1277 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2355">18</a></sup>, the root age of the tree was calibrated to 48â52âMa.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Gene family analysis</h3><p>Gene families were identified based on protein-coding sequences of <i>Populus trichocarpa, P. alba</i> var. <i>pyramidalis</i>, <i>P. euphratica, P. deltoides, Salix suchowensis</i>, and <i>S. purpurea</i>. We generated a maximum-likelihood gene tree using RaxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688â2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2379">50</a></sup> with the GTRGAMMA model based on 3040 single-copy orthologous genes from the aforementioned Sal-SP. We then determined expanded and rapidly evolved orthologous gene families using CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W. C., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2384">51</a></sup> with default parameters based on the gene tree inferred among the 13,793 shared gene families of the Salicaceae species. The lineage/species-specific gene families were determined according to the presence or absence of genes for a given lineage/species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">GO and KEGG enrichment analysis</h3><p>GO and KEGG enrichment analyses were performed using the <i>R</i> package clusterProfiler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Yu, G., Wang, L.-G., Han, Y. &amp; He, Q.-Y. clusterProfiler: an R package for comparing biological themes among gene clusters. OMICS 16, 284â287 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2399">52</a></sup> to identify significantly enriched terms. We used expanded/rapidly evolved/species-specific genes as input and all genes of the whole genome annotated with GO and KEGG terms as background for each species examined. The resulting <i>p</i> values were corrected for multiple comparisons using the method of Benjamini and Hochberg.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Anthocyanin measurement</h3><p>Anthocyanin content of Cushion willow branchlets of greenhouse LJ_BS (elevation: 2950 m) and LJ_LS (elevation: 3950 m) plants (6 individuals for each group) was measured according to Teng et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Teng, S., Keurentjes, J., Bentsink, L., Koornneef, M. &amp; Smeekens, S. Sucrose-specific induction of anthocyanin biosynthesis in Arabidopsis requires the MYB75/PAP1 gene. Plant Physiol. 139, 1840â1852 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2414">53</a></sup> (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">2</a>). Two measures of absorbance of extracts were made at 535 and 650ânm by spectrophotometry with anthocyanin quantity equaling (A535-A650) per gram fresh weight.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Whole-genome resequencing and SNP calling</h3><p>We obtained plant material of individuals from 14 wild populations in localities across the distribution area of Cushion willow (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM11">7</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#Fig1">1</a>). One of these individuals was from Tianbao Mountain, Shangri-La Country, used to produce the assembled reference genome sequence as described above. The genomes of the remaining 77 individuals were resequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform. Paired-end reads from each of these individuals were aligned to the assembled Cushion willow reference genome using BWA with default parameters. To minimize the influence of mapping bias, we further discarded the following sites: (1) sites with extremely low (&lt;300Ã across all samples, i.e. less than an average of 4Ã per sample) or extremely high coverage (&gt;3000Ã, or approximately twice the mean depth at variant sites) across all samples after investigating the coverage distribution empirically; (2) sites with a high number of reads (on average &gt;8 reads per sample) with mapping score equaling zero; (3) sites located within repetitive sequences as identified using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Smit A. F. A., Hubley R., Green P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. (2013â2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2436">39</a></sup>; and (4) sites belonging to organelle genomes, and the 9 contigs that were not anchored on the 19 super-scaffolds. 196,214,630 sites were retained after the above steps. SNP calling was then conducted using Freebayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Garrison E., Marth G. T. Haplotype-based variant detection from short-read sequencing. Preprint at &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1207.3907&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2440">54</a></sup> to call sites with Quality &gt;20. We performed several filtering steps to minimize SNP calling bias using VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156â2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2444">55</a></sup> and BCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Narasimhan, V. et al. BCFtools/RoH: a hidden Markov model approach for detecting autozygosity from next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 32, 1749â1751 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2449">56</a></sup> and to retain only high-quality SNPs. These included: (1) removal of SNPs at sites not passing all aforementioned filtering criteria; (2) retention of only bi-allelic SNPs with a distance of &gt;5 bp away from any indels; and (3) treating genotypes with quality score &lt;20 and depth &lt;5 as missing. Finally, we removed SNPs with missing rate &gt;20%, which resulted in 6,524,760 SNPs. The 1,595,067 SNPs with minor allele frequency &lt;5% were deemed as high<b>-</b>quality SNPs and were used for downstream analysis. SNPs were annotated using ANNOVAR (<a href="https://vatlab.github.io/vat-docs/documentation/tutorials/annovar/">https://vatlab.github.io/vat-docs/documentation/tutorials/annovar/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Genetic structure and gene flow analysis</h3><p>Genetic structure based on SNP variation among the sequenced 78 genotypes was analyzed using fastSTRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Raj, A., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. fastSTRUCTURE: variational inference of population structure in large SNP data sets. Genetics 197, 573â589 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2468">57</a></sup> with 2â15 ancestral clusters (<i>K</i>) and the value <i>K</i>â=â7 was selected using the chooseK.py function. A neighbor-joining tree was constructed using MEGA7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 7.0 for bigger datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1870â1874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2478">58</a></sup> with <i>p</i>-distance method and the clade supports were calculated using 1000 bootstraps. Principal component analysis (PCA) was performed with GCTA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Yang, J., Lee, S. H., Goddard, M. E. &amp; Visscher, P. M. GCTA: a tool for genome-wide complex trait analysis. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 88, 76â82 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2486">59</a></sup>. To estimate gene flow between populations of Cushion willow, we used Treemix v1.13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pickrell, J. K. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002967 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2490">27</a></sup> conducted on the ~1.6âM high-quality SNP dataset, with the settings -se -bootstrap -k 500 -m, and values for (âm) ranging from 1 to 10.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">LD analysis and calculation of population parameters</h3><p>We estimated LD decay based on the coefficient of determination (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) between any two loci using PopLDdecay (<a href="https://github.com/BGI-shenzhen/PopLDdecay">https://github.com/BGI-shenzhen/PopLDdecay</a>). Tajimaâs <i>D</i>, <i>F</i>st, and <i>Î¸</i><sub><i>Ï</i></sub> were calculated from sample allele frequency likelihoods in ANGSD over non-overlapping 2-kb windows. A summary of results is listed in Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM18">14</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32">Site ancestral state estimation</h3><p>We inferred the site ancestral state (SAS) of Cushion willow following the Enredo-Pecan-Ortheus pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Paten, B. et al. Genome-wide nucleotide-level mammalian ancestor reconstruction. Genome Res. 18, 1829â1843 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2534">60</a></sup>, a probabilistic alignment-based method. We constructed a NJ tree using MEGA, including Cushion willow, <i>S. purpurea</i> [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR3927001">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR3927001</a>], <i>S. suchowensis</i> [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR8255666">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR8255666</a>], and <i>S. viminalis</i> [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=ERR1558611">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=ERR1558611</a>], with <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> as outgroup to define the root. We identified 249,212,838 high confidence and 53,624,778 low confidence sites, yielding 302,837,616 (89.2%) sites as ancestral state in the assembled Cushion willow genome, which was used to estimate unfolded SFS and polarize SNPs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Divergence and demographic history of populations</h3><p>We inferred the divergence times of six population pairs of Cushion willow (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM15">11</a>) using a coalescent simulation-based method implemented in fastsimcoal 2.6, which compared 22 demographic models (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">13</a>) using SFS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Liu, X. M. &amp; Fu, Y. X. Exploring population size changes using SNP frequency spectra. Nat. Genet. 47, 555â559 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2577">61</a></sup>. Two-dimensional joint SFS (2D-SFS) was constructed from posterior probabilities of sample allele frequencies by realSFS implemented in ANGSD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Korneliussen, T. S., Albrechtsen, A. &amp; Nielsen, R. ANGSD: analysis of next generation sequencing data. BMC Bioinform. 15, 356 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2581">62</a></sup>. Only reads with mapping quality &gt;30 and bases with quality score &gt;20 were used in the analysis. To minimize the influence of mapping bias, sites not passing the aforementioned filtering criteria were removed. Sites within gene regions were also removed. 102,494,073 sites were therefore retained. A total of 100,000 coalescent simulations were used for the estimation of the expected 2D-SFS and log-likelihood for a set of demographic parameters in each model. Mutation rate (<i>Î¼</i>), shown to be related to genome size in a variety of organisms including <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, was calculated following Lynch (2010)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lynch, M. Evolution of the mutation rate. Trends Genet. 26, 345â352 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2592">63</a></sup> and estimated to be 1.01âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> mutations per site per generation. All parameter estimates were global ML estimates from 50 independent fastsimcoal2 runs, with 100,000 simulations per likelihood estimation and 40 cycles of the likelihood maximization algorithm. The best model was identified through the Akaikeâs information criterion; simulated datasets were compared with the observed site frequency spectra to evaluate the fit of the best demographic model. Parameter confidence intervals of the best model were obtained by 100 parametric bootstraps, with 50 independent runs in each bootstrap. Divergence times were estimated for generation times of 3, 10, and 15 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Smart, L. et al. Genetic improvement of shrub willow (Salix spp.) crops for bioenergy and environmental applications in the United States. Unasylva 56, 51â55 (2005)." href="#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2598">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ikeda, H., Yakubov, V., Barkalov, V. &amp; Setoguchi, H. Post-glacial East Asian origin of the alpine shrub Phyllodoce aleutica (Ericaceae) in Beringia. J. Biogeogr. 45, 1261â1274 (2018)." href="#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2598_1">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wang, J., Street, N. R., Scofield, D. G. &amp; Ingvarsson, P. K. Variation in linked selection and recombination drive genomic divergence during allopatric speciation of European and American aspens. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1754â1767 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2601">66</a></sup>.</p><p>The demographic history of six of the genetic groups identified by structure analysis was examined using a stairway plot with 200 bootstrap iterations, to infer changes in population size over time using SFS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Liu, X. M. &amp; Fu, Y. X. Exploring population size changes using SNP frequency spectra. Nat. Genet. 47, 555â559 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2608">61</a></sup>. The EY group was excluded because it comprised only three individuals. Ten individuals, which did not cluster according to geography and were admixed, were also excluded from analysis. SFS was estimated using ANGSD based on SAS as above. The same mutation rate as above was used in analyses, with analyses repeated across the same range of generation times, i.e. 3, 10, and 15 years.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Environment heterogeneity analysis</h3><p>We obtained four bioclimatic factors, annual mean air temperature, air temperature annual range, annual precipitation, and precipitation seasonality, from Worldclim (<a href="https://www.worldclim.org/">https://www.worldclim.org/</a>) with spatial resolution of 1âkm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Hijmans, R. J., Cameron, S. E., Parra, J. L., Jones, P. G. &amp; Jarvis, A. Very high resolution interpolated climate surfaces for global land areas. Int. J. Climatol. 25, 1965â1978 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2624">67</a></sup> (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM19">15</a>). Terrestrial ecosystem heterogeneity data of global habitat with resolution of 1âkm based on textural features of Enhanced Vegetation Index (EVI) imagery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Tuanmu, M. N. &amp; Jetz, W. A global, remote sensing-based characterization of terrestrial habitat heterogeneity for biodiversity and ecosystem modelling. Glob. Ecol. Biogeogr. 24, 1329â1339 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2631">68</a></sup> was obtained. We chose eight environmental factors, coefficient of variation (CV), dissimilarity, evenness, homogeneity, range, Shannon, Simpson, and standard deviation (Std) from the dataset, and conducted independent-samples <i>t</i> tests on these variables to examine environmental differences between lower (2760â3230âm) and high (3800â4430âm) elevation sites occupied by Cushion willow (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM16">12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec35">Selective sweep analysis</h3><p>We used SweepFinder2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="DeGiorgio, M., Huber, C. D., Hubisz, M. J., Hellmann, I. &amp; Nielsen, R. SWEEPFINDER2: Increased sensitivity, robustness and flexibility. Bioinformatics 32, 1895â1897 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2651">69</a></sup>, a composite likelihood ratio statistic (CLR) to search for signs of selective sweeps in populations from high (LJ_XF and LJ_LS, nine individuals) versus lower (LJ_GH and LJ_BS, nine individuals) elevations on the same mountain in Lijiang County (LJ) based on SAS. The small fraction of SNPs that could not be polarized was excluded from further analysis. We only used sites that were polymorphic or that represented fixed substitutions in each group of populations to scan for sweeps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Huber, C. D., Degiorgio, M., Hellmann, I. &amp; Nielsen, R. Detecting recent selective sweeps while controlling for mutation rate and background selection. Mol. Ecol. 25, 142â156 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2655">70</a></sup>. Sweepfinder2 was run with a grid size of 2âkb. CLR scores were merged into sweep regions if neighboring scores exceeded CLR scores of 100. To examine the threshold for CLR scores, we used neutral simulations obtained from the best demographic model between the high and lower elevation LJ populations, which was model 18 (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">13</a>) as inferred by fastsimcoal2.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Excoffier, L., Dupanloup, I., Huerta-Sanchez, E., Sousa, V. C. &amp; Foll, M. Robust demographic inference from genomic and SNP data. PLoS Genet. 9, e1003905 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3257e2662">28</a></sup>. We then simulated 100 datasets for each of the high and lower elevation populations by fastsimcoal2.6 for sweep analysis using SweepFinder2. The 95th quantile of values composed by maximum CLR score of each simulation dataset was used as CLR threshold for identifying sweep region, which was 38.9 and 30.9 in high and lower elevation populations, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec36">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and associatedÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as aÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The whole genome shotgun project for Cushion willow is deposited in DDBJ/ENA/GenBank and GWH (Genome Warehouse) under the accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/VDCV00000000">VDCV00000000</a> and <a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh/Assembly/663/show">GWHAAZH00000000</a>, respectively. All raw reads generated in this study are deposited in the NCBI database under BioProject accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA472210">PRJNA472210</a> (Cushion willow related raw reads) and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA545026">PRJNA545026</a> (RNAseq raw reads for <i>Salix souliei, S. tetrasperma, S. wallichiana</i>, and <i>S. babylonica</i>). The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM20">3d</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">11</a>d and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">17</a>, and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13128-y#MOESM1">1</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>
